The language takes a fair bit of deciphering. It's jarring prose,</span>
  <span id="freeText15689613285973603724" style="display:none">What a nasty,ugly and unpalatable book. But it's not an easy one to forget about,thanks to these very attributes.<br /><br />On the one hand,it is supposedly a cult classic. On the other hand,it faced obscenity charges,and is very controversial. The author was a drug user,he was sick,and he was pretty desperate.<br />He figured that he knew the alphabet,so he might as well try his hand at writing.<br /><br />It's not pretty writing,there are expletives galore. The language takes a fair bit of deciphering. It's jarring prose,it is downright offensive. It presents the ugly side of life,and does so with a vengeance.<br /><br />The subject matter including gang rape,drug use,paedophilia etc. is none too appealing. However,there is one thing to be said for this book,such nasty things do happen in reality.<br /><br /> I read one story,it shocked me alright. I tried skimming the rest,I couldn't. I read a more sanitised summary instead. I hated this book,and yet I wouldn't forget it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15689613285973603724" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_127201326" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/127201326" />
    <a title="Jake" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1821338-jake"><img alt="Jake" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1229910175p2/1821338.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/127201326?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jake" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jake" href="/user/show/1821338-jake">Jake</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780747574590" href="/book/show/52077.Last_Exit_to_Brooklyn">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1821338-jake?shelf=novel">novel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer127201326" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2204021296093330381">I grew up in Brooklyn, and I live here now- so people are sometimes impressed by the length of my tenure and my selection of "back in the early 1980s" stories. At least until they realize that I'm from Park Slope, which is like being from the Upper West Side of Manhattan- sure, it probably had its rough spots, but no one is ever going to give you credit for surviving the rough streets of Riverside Drive. This is particularly true when you run into someone else who grew up in Brooklyn, and play t</span>
  <span id="freeText2204021296093330381" style="display:none">I grew up in Brooklyn, and I live here now- so people are sometimes impressed by the length of my tenure and my selection of "back in the early 1980s" stories. At least until they realize that I'm from Park Slope, which is like being from the Upper West Side of Manhattan- sure, it probably had its rough spots, but no one is ever going to give you credit for surviving the rough streets of Riverside Drive. This is particularly true when you run into someone else who grew up in Brooklyn, and play the "so what neighborhood are you from" game. In terms of hard-core-ness, pretty much every neighborhood beats Park Slope- except maybe Brooklyn Heights. Neighborhoods that do particularly well in this game include Sunset Park, Bensonhurst, East New York, the attractively named Gravesend, and Bushwick. <br /><br />"Last Exit to Brooklyn" is a good illustration of why these neighborhoods win. It's primarily set at the edge of Sunset Park, by the Army Terminal, in the late 1940s and early 1950s, and also in the Red Hook Houses. At first I was reminded of Graham Greene's Brighton Rock, which also concerns the lives of the the lower class, but unlike Graham Greene, Selby doesn't show much empathy toward his characters, or bother to give them any redeeming qualities. That allows him to create a hell of sickening intensity- all trannies turning rough trade, street hustlers gang raping whores, and parents in the Projects neglecting their kids while practicing alcoholism and unsafe sex. <br /><br />He is particularly acute on issues of sex, race, and class. The most searing story in the collection is "The Strike", which concerns an incredibly pathetic union organizer using strike funds to pay for his adventures with gay men. As in all the other stories, there is no hero: the protagonist is a wife-beater, a thief, and ultimately a child-rapist- and the other people he mixes with aren't much better. Which is to say that this isn't exactly an easy read, and it's made even harder by Selby's eccentric spelling and grammar. It's the kind of book that leaves you feeling kind of messed up and gives you bad dreams, but which seems important if you want to understand how the world (or Brooklyn) really works. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2204021296093330381" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1134362591" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1134362591" />
    <a title="Annie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38036772-annie"><img alt="Annie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586205974p2/38036772.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1134362591?book_show_action=true">Dec 17, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Annie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Annie" href="/user/show/38036772-annie">Annie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38036772-annie?shelf=let-s-get-high">let-s-get-high</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38036772-annie?shelf=reread">reread</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1134362591" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17579550410539443948">Easily one of the most scarring books I've ever had the fortune (yes, you read that right) to read. This book is like a brief trip to hell. It's full of the absolute worst humans imaginable- cruel, brutish, stupid, common, empty meat-suits- and yet you get no pleasure from their screams. In fact, their lack of humanity makes their horrifying lives even worse, because they don't even seem to know how appalling their circumstances are. This book combines the drawn-out frustration of Kafka's Castle</span>
  <span id="freeText17579550410539443948" style="display:none">Easily one of the most scarring books I've ever had the fortune (yes, you read that right) to read. This book is like a brief trip to hell. It's full of the absolute worst humans imaginable- cruel, brutish, stupid, common, empty meat-suits- and yet you get no pleasure from their screams. In fact, their lack of humanity makes their horrifying lives even worse, because they don't even seem to know how appalling their circumstances are. This book combines the drawn-out frustration of Kafka's Castle with the senseless violence of Burgess's A Clockwork Orange, except twice as gruesome. <br /><br />Halfway through the book, I'm thinking "Why am I reading this? Why don't I just put it down?" But I really hate giving up on books halfway through- so many books redeem themselves entirely in the last few pages- so I powered through, hoping this was one such book.<br /><br />It was not.<br /><br />Relieved to have finished this book which had made my own life look bleaker somehow (Selby is gifted at this), I put it away and pick up a rather less soul-sucking book. But I found myself thinking about this book still, because it was still really bothering me- like having my heart rubbed on a cheese grater, seriously. And about a week later, an idea occurred to me.<br /><br />What if Selby wrote this book not because he's a pervert who enjoys writing pretty language around nauseating sadism, not because he's some heartless creep... but because he *isn't*, and neither are we? What if he wrote this at a time in his life when he was feeling numb to his own humanity, and wrote this to provoke a reaction in himself? To create this scene utterly devoid of humanity, in order to remind himself of his own humanity, that he could still have these heart-rending reactions to human suffering, even if those humans were despicable themselves? What if he wrote it to remind us that we can still feel, still react with empathy for suffering and recoil from things worthy of disgust? <br /><br />With that in mind, I rethought the entire book, and what had been a harrowing trial to read became something of a heartening psychological journey for me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17579550410539443948" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_314367860" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/314367860" />
    <a title="Chris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6316008-chris"><img alt="Chris" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600893038p2/6316008.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/314367860?book_show_action=true">Apr 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris" href="/user/show/6316008-chris">Chris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/13345041-last-exit-to-brooklyn">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6316008-chris?shelf=2012-reads">2012-reads</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer314367860" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14059656996331328976">I can picture this book being read in college literature classes. I am sure that it deserves its place in modern American Literature and I am also sure that this book and Selby have their fans. I won't dispute his genius. My rating is not based on the "merit" of the book, but on whether I liked it and the truth is that I found this book to be repulsive and nauseating. I think that I was expecting it to be sort of like Kennedy's Iron Weed (which I liked) but much darker but Last Exit isn't dark--</span>
  <span id="freeText14059656996331328976" style="display:none">I can picture this book being read in college literature classes. I am sure that it deserves its place in modern American Literature and I am also sure that this book and Selby have their fans. I won't dispute his genius. My rating is not based on the "merit" of the book, but on whether I liked it and the truth is that I found this book to be repulsive and nauseating. I think that I was expecting it to be sort of like Kennedy's Iron Weed (which I liked) but much darker but Last Exit isn't dark---it is more like wallowing in a sewer and I kept reading only by hoping to see what Selby was trying to say to the reader. I kept looking for insight into the human condition. I must confess that in the end I just didn't see the point. I found the loosely related stories that make up this "novel" to be repetitive. Follow a repellent and completely repulsive character around, watch them victimize people, and then watch them subjected to extreme violence at the hands of other equally repellent and repulsive characters. As much as I hated reading this book one thought never left my mind. I felt sorry for Selby. He must have lived with a terrible darkness inside him to have written this book. The scary thing is that as bad as this book is, his novel The Room is supposed to be much darker. Looking at the reviews I see one person stating that they felt like burning their copy---and they LIKED the book! That's a common thread. Even the people that give his books 5 stars didn't enjoy reading them. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14059656996331328976" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_201086819" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/201086819" />
    <a title="bobbygw  " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2692376-bobbygw"><img alt="bobbygw  " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1251838122p2/2692376.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/201086819?book_show_action=true">Aug 22, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="bobbygw  " class="user" itemprop="url" name="bobbygw  " href="/user/show/2692376-bobbygw">bobbygw  </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">literary classics, 20th century fiction, American existentialism</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2692376-bobbygw?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2692376-bobbygw?shelf=re-read">re-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2692376-bobbygw?shelf=literary-classics">literary-classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer201086819" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17221297490692843839">Controversy has always surrounded Selby, Jr.'s writing. From the start, with Last Exit (being his first novel), his original UK publisher Calder and Boyers faced government prosecution in 1967, under the 1959 Obscene Publications Act. It was a major trial, especially as it was originally found guilty of being `obscene', and because, more importantly, the Appeal in 1968 overturned that decision and paved the way for a much more open-minded interpretation of literature as to merit.<br/><br/>In some intervie</span>
  <span id="freeText17221297490692843839" style="display:none">Controversy has always surrounded Selby, Jr.'s writing. From the start, with Last Exit (being his first novel), his original UK publisher Calder and Boyers faced government prosecution in 1967, under the 1959 Obscene Publications Act. It was a major trial, especially as it was originally found guilty of being `obscene', and because, more importantly, the Appeal in 1968 overturned that decision and paved the way for a much more open-minded interpretation of literature as to merit.<br /><br />In some interviews, and in one introduction to the novel that he wrote, Selby himself said that when he was writing this novel (six years in the making), he `was only aware of the rage and anger within' (quoted from the QPB edition in 1994). He also acknowledged that, while he has no favourite among his novels, he singles out The Room, and then this novel, notably, because through it he `truly did learn how to write' (quote from same edition).<br /><br />Set in New York in the late 1950s, the story grabs you from the start, conveying an incredibly raw, visceral, yet always disturbingly poetic quality throughout. It is superb for its genuine grittiness, horribly grim reality and ugliness of modern inner city life - of thought, attitude, action and feeling - both projected outwards, as well as internalised, all of which is captured through numerous voices of the dispossessed, alienated, disenfranchised. Selby is in the tradition of naturalist/realist fiction, but he goes a significant step further, in that, often through his first-person character narratives/viewpoints, he achieves an hallucinatory quality whose intensity strengthens the realities he conveys.<br /><br />One noteworthy critic, James R. Giles, in his excellent study of Selby's works, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/46946.Understanding_Hubert_Selby_JR_" title="Understanding Hubert Selby JR. by James Richard Giles" rel="nofollow noopener">Understanding Hubert Selby, Jr.</a>, wrote `It is true that, while all the characters in the novel are victimized by a brutal environment, they are guilty of moral failures that make their victimization complete and irrevocable', and this is done by Selby's exploring without fear or judgement powerful themes that resonant as much today as they did on first publication in 1964 in the US: drug addiction, misogyny, violence of rape and beatings, prostitution, the relentless drudgery and alienation of most jobs as well as unemployment, without value or meaning and life in general that is suffused with hate and a distressingly dark, twisted humour (when not expressed through seething resentment or explosive anger).<br /><br />I reckon, Dante would have regarded Last Exit as a worthy match to his own vision of Hell - especially through characters who represent the sick underbelly of the city: self-deluding, beaten-up prostitutes, incredibly violent youths and gangs, a desperately lonely, elderly woman who has no life other than pathetic memories of her dead husband and son, among others. As for disturbing, truly dark humour, one such example will give you a powerful sense of it: at one point, two women sitting chatting on a bench in a miserable New York housing project, joke about and look forward with sick, twisted glee to the prospect of a baby - crawling on an upper-storey tenement window-ledge - falling to its death: they're disappointed that it is saved in the nick of time).<br /><br />In particular, the lives of a handful of individuals are portrayed with great psychological depth, narrated most often from the first-person viewpoint, in a stream-of-consciousness fashion that remains clear, coherent and compelling. These viewpoints capture their desperation, self-loathing, hatred and confusion about themselves and their environments: defiant yet self-deluding Georgette, a hip drag queen who is pathetically in love with Vinnie and convinced she can change him for the better and that he will truly love her; while he himself is a psychopathic and sociopathic gang leader interested in only in sadistic and often instant gratification; Tralala, a violently angry, predatory prostitute who ultimately is destroyed in the most horrifying way imaginable; Harry - interestingly, that name is ubiquitous among male characters in Selby's fiction, acting as a synonym for the type of man who is misogynistic, dispossessed, angry, self-loathing, and self-deluding - who is a trade union leader, loathsome, selfish, arrogant and boring, who is despised or at best tolerated by his co-workers - and, worse, whose misogyny is so genuinely convincing and disturbing, which we hear, being `inside his head', listening trapped to his banal voice, desperation, loathing and perspective, such that it makes Bateman's misogyny in American Psycho appear not only over the top, but utterly unreal (and never mind Ellis' darkly satirical intentions).<br /><br />Selby has been described by the literature academic and critic, Josephine Hendin, as a `clinician of violence ... whose novels have the immediacy of art' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1780093.Vulnerable_People_A_View_Of_American_Fiction_Since_1945" title="Vulnerable People A View Of American Fiction Since 1945 by Josephine Hendin" rel="nofollow noopener">Vulnerable People: A View of American Fiction Since 1945</a>, which I think is true; likewise, he is a literary master of demonstrating through his characters a moral ugliness, misogyny and existential despair, and whose power as a novelist is unmatched and unprecedented in fiction (Sartre's famous Nausea (Penguin Modern Classics), for example, is a happy walk in the park compared to Selby's vision). <br /><br />He is a truly remarkable writer, and, while he wrote six novels in his lifetime, I believe the most powerful and compelling (while not the darkest), remains Last Exit. I cannot recommend it highly enough - it is a genuine work of art, but its power is dark and troubling, so I would likewise strongly recommend you stay clear of this novel if you find yourself in a depressed frame of mind...</span>
  <a data-text-id="17221297490692843839" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_694531687" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/694531687" />
    <a title="Jason" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3401177-jason"><img alt="Jason" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1441736984p2/3401177.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/694531687?book_show_action=true">Aug 14, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason" href="/user/show/3401177-jason">Jason</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3401177-jason?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3401177-jason?shelf=ebook">ebook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3401177-jason?shelf=odd">odd</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3401177-jason?shelf=not-worth-finishing">not-worth-finishing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3401177-jason?shelf=2013">2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer694531687" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8951571448240007053">The high ratings and high praise for this book put me in mind of the following scenario: a group of people stand around a display at a gallery - simply, a pile of shit upon a table. The idiots surrounding the table do not dare to let the others know their hidden truth: they don't (don/t) get it, it looks like shit to them! No one wants to be the first and possibly look the fool, so they begin to ascribe to it those catch-phrase buzzwords they've heard others use in similar situations. Brutal! Tr</span>
  <span id="freeText8951571448240007053" style="display:none">The high ratings and high praise for this book put me in mind of the following scenario: a group of people stand around a display at a gallery - simply, a pile of shit upon a table. The idiots surrounding the table do not dare to let the others know their hidden truth: they don't (don/t) get it, it looks like shit to them! No one wants to be the first and possibly look the fool, so they begin to ascribe to it those catch-phrase buzzwords they've heard others use in similar situations. Brutal! Truth! Avant! Garde! and they all turn and nod and pat each other on the back for being so very smart and hip.<br /><br />Folks, it ain't art, IT'S SHIT.<br /><br />The dialogue is such a headache-inducing mess that if I ever came across people that actually spoke in such wretched patter I'd cut their damn tongues out. This pales in comparison to the grammatical butchery that makes my own run-on sentences look like unfinished thoughts. The author mistakes his aversion to periods and paragraph breaks for the real flow of conversation. SHIT! Replacing apostrophes with /'s? Is this how cool you are Mr. Selby? SHIT. <br /><br />Like the drag queens who treat us to an unrealistic evening (speed consricts blood vessels, erections need blood, just sayin') this piece looks like a book from the outside, but inside?<br /><br />SHIT!</span>
  <a data-text-id="8951571448240007053" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1002456375" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8839744101802150216">Brutal and haunting.<br/><br/>I read one or two of the sections of this book years ago in <i>Evergreen Review</i> and then I read the entire book. That was many years ago, but the writing is still imprinted in my mind. This would either be a five star book - it's remained with me for all of these years - or a one star - I can't imagine wanting to read it again.<br/>I'll go with five stars because any book that has that kind of effect on me deserves five.<br/><br/></span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('9Nvt4kizBDNCeft4b82qIU6yrUj6lBMjmpQrJJ/9r5fO/YQw8+3NW+VSTxmYOKD+mO1KgDQZnUTB6m2VUr5dDQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('b5koP1GxQxyrqjaWZLVynyR+b47SFC7PVDELRPnHUstVv0Ht6u+KdAyBgveTQHhA8iGIRhyZoKgPT031NISgUQ==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/26335.Hubert_Selby_Jr_">About Hubert Selby Jr.</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/26335.Hubert_Selby_Jr_"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1397502493p3/26335.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/26335.Hubert_Selby_Jr_">Hubert Selby Jr.
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,963
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4570537269446993606">Hubert Selby, Jr. was born in Brooklyn and went to sea as a merchant marine while still in his teens. Laid low by lung disease, he was, after a decade of hospitalizations, written off as a goner and sent home to die. Deciding instead to live, but having no way to make a living, he came to a realization that would change the course of literature: "I knew the alphabet. Maybe I could be a writer." Dr</span>
  <span id="freeText4570537269446993606" style="display:none">Hubert Selby, Jr. was born in Brooklyn and went to sea as a merchant marine while still in his teens. Laid low by lung disease, he was, after a decade of hospitalizations, written off as a goner and sent home to die. Deciding instead to live, but having no way to make a living, he came to a realization that would change the course of literature: "I knew the alphabet. Maybe I could be a writer." Drawing from the soul of his Brooklyn neighborhood, he began writing something called "The Queen Is Dead," which evolved, after six years, into his first novel, <i>Last Exit to Brooklyn</i> (1964), a book that Allen Ginsberg predicted would "explode like a rusty hellish bombshell over America and still be eagerly read in a hundred years."<br /><br />Selby's second novel, <i>The Room</i> (1971), considered by some to be his masterpiece, received, as Selby said, "the greatest reviews I've ever read in my life," then rapidly vanished leaving barely a trace of its existence. Over the years, however, especially in Europe, <i>The Room</i> has come to be recognized as what Selby himself perceives it to be: the most disturbing book ever written, a book that he himself was unable to read again for twenty years after writing it.<br /><br />"A man obsessed / is a man possessed / by a demon." Thus the defining epigraph of <i>The Demon</i> (1976), a novel that, like <i>The Room</i>, has been better understood and more widely embraced abroad than at home.<br /><br />If <i>The Room</i> is Selby's own favorite among his books, <i>Requiem for a Dream</i> (1978) contains his favorite opening line: "Harry locked his mother in the closet." It is perhaps the truest and most horrific tale of heroin addiction ever written. <br /><br /><i>Song of the Silent Snow</i> (1986) brought together fifteen stories whose writing spanned more than twenty years.<br /><br />Selby continued to write short fiction, screenplays and teleplays at his apartment in West Hollywood. His work appeared in many journals, including <i>Yugen, Black Mountain Review, Evergreen Review, Provincetown Review, Kulchur, New Directions Annual, Swank</i> and <i>Open City</i>. For the last 20 years of his life, Selby taught creative writing as an adjunct professor in the Master of Professional Writing program at the University of Southern California. Selby often wryly noted that The New York Times would not review his books when they were published, but he predicted that they'd print his obituary.<br /><br />The movie <i>Last Exit to Brooklyn,</i> Directed by Uli Edel, was made in 1989 and his 1978 novel <i>Requiem for a Dream</i> was made into a film that was released in 2000. Selby himself had a small role as a prison guard. <br /><br />In the 1980s, Selby made the acquaintance of rock singer Henry Rollins, who had long admired Selby's works and publicly championed them. Rollins not only helped broaden Selby's readership, but also arranged recording sessions and reading tours for Selby. Rollins issued original recordings through his own 2.13.61 publications, and distributed Selby's other works.<br /><br />During the last years of his life, Selby suffered from depression and fits of rage, but was always a caring father and grandfather. The last month of his life Selby spent in and out of the hospital. He died in Highland Park, Los Angeles, California of chronic obstructive pulmonary lung disease. Selby was survived by his wife of 35 years, Suzanne; four children and 11 grandchildren.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4570537269446993606" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

This partial loads on almost every page view.  The associated React component makes
a call to SignInPromptController#get to determine if the user should see the sign in interstial.
This is determined by how many signed out pagehits the user has executed an how recently they have
last seen the insterstitial.  If the controller responds indicating the popup should appear, the
